Use [[Special:ApiFeatureUsage]] to see usage of deprecated features by your application."},"revisions":{"*":"Because \"rvslots\" was not specified, a legacy format has been used for the output. This format is deprecated, and in the future the new format will always be used."}},"query":{"normalized":[{"from":"Clarence_White","to":"Clarence White"}],"pages":{"1253793":{"pageid":1253793,"ns":0,"title":"Clarence White","revisions":[{"contentformat":"text/x-wiki","contentmodel":"wikitext","*":"{{Short description|American musician (1944\u201373)}}\n{{Other people}}\n{{Infobox musical artist\n| name            = Clarence White\n| image           = Clarence White01.jpg\n| caption         = White in 1970\n| image_size      =\n| birth_name      = Clarence Joseph LeBlanc\n| alias           =\n| birth_date      = June 7, 1944\n| birth_place     = [[Lewiston, Maine]], U.S.\n| death_date      = {{death date and age|1973|7|15|1944|6|7}}<ref name=\"hjort6\">{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|author-link=Christopher Hjort|page=321|year=2008|title=So You Want to Be a Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-by-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>\n| death_place     = [[Palmdale, California]], U.S.\n| instrument      = [[Guitar]], [[mandolin]], [[vocals]]\n| genre           = [[Bluegrass music|Bluegrass]], [[Country music|country]], [[country rock]], [[Rock music|rock]]\n| occupation      = Musician, singer\n| years_active    = 1954\u20131973\n| label           = Sundown, Republic, Briar International, [[Pacific Jazz Records|World Pacific]], Bakersfield International, [[Columbia Records|Columbia]], [[Warner Bros. Records|Warner Bros.]]\n| associated_acts = [[Kentucky Colonels (band)|The Kentucky Colonels]], [[Nashville West]], [[The Byrds]], [[Muleskinner (band)|Muleskinner]]\n| website         = \n}}\n\n'''Clarence White''' (born '''Clarence Joseph LeBlanc'''; June 7, 1944 \u2013 July 15, 1973)<ref name=\"hjort6\"/> was an American [[Bluegrass music|bluegrass]] and [[country music|country]] [[guitarist]] and singer.<ref name=\"hjort\">{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|author-link=Christopher Hjort|page=14|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref><ref name=\"allmusic\">{{cite web|last=Erlewine|first=Stephen Thomas|author-link=Stephen Thomas Erlewine|title=Clarence White biography|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|url={{AllMusic|class=artist|id=mn0000136588/biography|pure_url=yes}}|access-date=2013-03-06}}</ref>  He is best known as a member of the bluegrass [[Musical ensemble|ensemble]] the [[The Kentucky Colonels (band)|Kentucky Colonels]] and the [[Rock music|rock]] band [[the Byrds]], as well as for being a pioneer of the musical genre of [[country rock]] during the late 1960s.<ref name=\"allmusic\"/> White also worked extensively as a [[session musician]], appearing on recordings by [[the Everly Brothers]], [[Joe Cocker]],<ref name=\"lightning\">{{cite AV media notes|title=White Lightnin'|others=Clarence White|year=2008|first=John|last=Delgatto|pages=2\u20135|type=CD booklet|publisher=Sierra Records|location=Pasadena}}</ref> [[Ricky Nelson]], [[Pat Boone]], [[the Monkees]], [[Randy Newman]],<ref name=\"timeless\">{{Cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|pages=267\u2013268|year=1998|title=The Byrds: Timeless Flight Revisited - The Sequel|edition=2nd|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=0-9529540-1-X}}</ref> [[Gene Clark]],<ref name=\"allmusic\"/> [[Linda Ronstadt]],<ref name=\"Kingsbury\">{{cite book|last=Kingsbury|first=Paul|title=The Encyclopedia of Country Music: The Ultimate Guide to the Music|url=https://archive.org/details/encyclopediaofco00coun|url-access=registration|year=1998|publisher=Oxford University Press, USA|location=New York City|isbn=0-19511-671-2|page=[https://archive.org/details/encyclopediaofco00coun/page/581 581]}}</ref> [[Arlo Guthrie]],<ref>{{cite book|last=Reineke|first=Hank|title=Arlo Guthrie: The Warner/Reprise Years|year=2012|publisher=Scarecrow Press|location=Lanham, MD|isbn=978-0-81088-331-4|pages=252\u2013253}}</ref> and [[Jackson Browne]] among others.<ref>{{Cite web|url=https://www.allmusic.com/album/jackson-browne-mw0000193102/credits|title=Jackson Browne album credits|website=[[AllMusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2017-12-30}}</ref>\n\nTogether with frequent collaborator [[Gene Parsons]], he invented the [[B-Bender]], a guitar accessory that enables a player to mechanically bend the B-string up a [[major second|whole tone]] and emulate the sound of a [[pedal steel guitar]]. White was inducted into the [[International Bluegrass Music Association]] Hall of Fame in 2016, and was inducted a second time in 2019 as a member of the Kentucky Colonels.\n\n==Early years==\nClarence Joseph LeBlanc was born on June 7, 1944, in [[Lewiston, Maine]].<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/> The LeBlanc family, who later changed their surname to White, were of French-Canadian ancestry and hailed from New Brunswick, Canada.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/><ref name=\"byrdwatcher\">{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Clarence White with the Kentucky Colonels: 1954-1965|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/memcw1.html|access-date=2013-08-13|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130903085652/http://ebni.com/byrds/memcw1.html|archive-date=2013-09-03}}</ref>  Clarence's father, Eric LeBlanc Sr., played [[Steel-string guitar|guitar]], [[banjo]], [[fiddle]], and [[harmonica]], ensuring that his offspring grew up surrounded by music.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/><ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/>  A child prodigy, Clarence began playing guitar at the age of six.<ref name=\"rogan\">{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|author-link=Johnny Rogan|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|page=469}}</ref>  At such a young age he was barely able to hold the instrument and as a result, he briefly switched to [[ukulele]], awaiting a time when his young hands would be big enough to confidently grapple with the guitar.<ref name=\"timeless\"/>\n\nIn 1954, when Clarence was ten, the White family relocated to Burbank, California and soon after, Clarence joined his brothers [[Roland White|Roland]] and Eric Jr. (who played [[mandolin]] and banjo respectively) in a trio called Three Little Country Boys.<ref name=\"hjort\"/>  Although they initially started out playing contemporary [[country music]], the group soon switched to a purely [[Bluegrass music|bluegrass]] repertoire, as a result of Roland's burgeoning interest in the genre.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/>\n\nIn 1957, banjoist Billy Ray Latham and [[Dobro]] player LeRoy Mack were added to the line-up,<ref name=\"timeless\"/> with the band renaming themselves the Country Boys soon after.<ref name=\"hjort\"/> In 1961, the Country Boys also added Roger Bush on double bass, as a replacement for Eric White Jr.<ref name=\"hjort\"/>  That same year, Clarence and other members of the Country Boys appeared on two episodes of ''[[The Andy Griffith Show]]''.<ref name=\"hjort\"/>  Between 1959 and 1962, the group released three [[Single (music)|singles]] on the Sundown, Republic, and Briar International [[record label]]s.<ref name=\"disc\">{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Clarence White Discography|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/refdiscogcw.html|access-date=2013-08-13|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130820140041/http://ebni.com/byrds/refdiscogcw.html|archive-date=2013-08-20}}</ref>\n\n==The Kentucky Colonels==\n{{main|Kentucky Colonels (band)}}\nFollowing the recording sessions for the Country Boys' debut album, the band changed its name to the [[Kentucky Colonels (band)|Kentucky Colonels]] in September 1962, at the suggestion of country guitarist and friend [[Joe Maphis]].<ref name=\"hjort\"/>  The band's album was released by Briar International under the title ''The New Sound of Bluegrass America'' in early 1963.<ref name=\"timeless2\">{{Cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|pages=581\u2013584|year=1998|title=The Byrds: Timeless Flight Revisited - The Sequel|edition=2nd|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=0-9529540-1-X}}</ref>\n\nAround this time, Clarence's [[flatpicking]] guitar style was becoming a much more prominent part of the group's music.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/>  After attending a performance by [[Doc Watson]] at the [[Ash Grove (music club)|Ash Grove]] folk club in Los Angeles, where he also met the guitarist, Clarence began to explore the possibilities of the acoustic guitar's role in bluegrass music.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/>  At that time, the guitar was largely regarded as a rhythm instrument in bluegrass, with only a few performers, such as  Watson, exploring its potential for soloing.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/>  White soon began to integrate elements of Watson's playing style\u2014including the use of open strings and syncopation\u2014into his own flatpicking guitar technique.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/>  His breathtaking speed and virtuosity on the instrument<ref name=\"timeless2\"/> was largely responsible for making the guitar a lead instrument within bluegrass.<ref>{{Cite magazine|url=https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-lists/best-guitarists-1234814010/clarence-white-3-1234814623/|title=250 Greatest Guitarists: #107 Clarence White|magazine=[[Rolling Stone]]|publisher=Wenner Media|access-date=2024-07-27}}</ref>\n\nThe Kentucky Colonels became well known on the bluegrass circuit during this period and made many live appearances throughout California and the United States.<ref name=\"hjort\"/>  Between bookings with the Colonels, White also made a guest appearance on [[Eric Weissberg]] and [[Marshall Brickman]]'s ''New Dimensions in Banjo & Bluegrass'' album, which would be re-released in 1973 as the [[soundtrack album]] to the film ''[[Deliverance]]'' (with Weissberg and Steve Mandell's version of \"[[Dueling Banjos]]\" added to the album's track listing).<ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/><ref>{{Cite web|url={{AllMusic|class=album|id=dueling-banjos-mw0000651271|pure_url=yes}}|title=\"Dueling Banjos\" from the Original Soundtrack Deliverance album review|last=Johnson|first=Zac|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-13}}</ref>\n\nIn 1964, the Colonels recruited fiddle player Bobby Sloan and continued to make live appearances at various clubs, concert halls and festivals.<ref name=\"hjort\"/> The Colonels' second album, ''[[Appalachian Swing!]]'',<ref name=\"timeless2\"/> was a commercial success and saw White's flatpicking permanently expand the language of bluegrass guitar.<ref name=\"hjort2\">{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=18|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref> Music critic Thom Owens has remarked that White's playing on the album, \"helped pioneer a new style in bluegrass; namely, he redefined the acoustic guitar as a solo instrument.\"<ref name=\"allmusic2\">{{Cite web|url={{AllMusic|class=album|id=appalachian-swing%21-mw0000706601|pure_url=yes}}|title=Appalachian Swing! album review|last=Owens|first=Thom|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-13}}</ref>\n\nShortly after the recording of the ''Appalachian Swing!'' album, Roland and Clarence undertook some [[Session musician|session work]] backing dobroist [[Tut Taylor]] on a Dobro-themed album that was released by World Pacific Records in late 1964 as ''Dobro Country''.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/>  Although the brothers were employed as session musicians, the album was credited to Tut Taylor, Roland and Clarence White upon release.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/>\n\nAlthough the Colonels were a successful recording act, it was becoming increasingly difficult for them to make a living, due to the waning popularity of the [[American folk music revival]] due to the [[British Invasion]] and homegrown [[folk rock]] acts, such as [[the Byrds]] and [[Bob Dylan]].<ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/>  As a result, the Colonels switched to [[electric instrument]]ation<ref name=\"byrdwatcher\"/> and hired a [[drummer]].<ref>{{Cite news|last=Delgatto|first=John|title=The Too-Short Career of Clarence White|pages=12\u201314|newspaper=Frets Magazine|publisher=GPI Publications|location=Cupertino, California|year=1986}}</ref>  In spite of these changes, the Kentucky Colonels dissolved as a band following a show on October 31, 1965.<ref name=\"timeless2\"/>  Clarence, Roland and Eric Jr. formed a new line-up of the Colonels in 1966, with several other musicians, but this second version of the group was short-lived and by early 1967 they had broken up.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher2\">{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Clarence White Plugs In: 1965-1968|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/memcw2.html|access-date=2013-08-14|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130730163100/http://ebni.com/byrds/memcw2.html|archive-date=2013-07-30}}</ref>\n\n==Session work (1966\u20131968)==\nDuring 1964, White began to look beyond bluegrass music towards [[rock 'n' roll]] as an avenue for artistic expression.<ref name=\"timeless\"/>  Although he was influenced by Country guitarists like [[Doc Watson]], [[Don Reno]] and [[Joe Maphis]], he also idolized the playing of [[jazz guitarist]] [[Django Reinhardt]], rock 'n' roller [[Chuck Berry]], and studio musician [[James Burton]].<ref name=\"byrdwatcher2\"/>  White even anticipated the viability of a folk/rock hybrid when, in the summer of 1964, he was approached by Jim Dickson to record a version of the then-unreleased Bob Dylan song \"Mr. Tambourine Man\" with electric instruments.<ref name=\"timeless\"/>  However, despite White's enthusiasm for the project, he was unable to convince his bandmates in the Kentucky Colonels of the experiment's validity<ref name=\"timeless\"/> and ultimately, the song was instead recorded by Dickson's proteges, the Byrds.<ref>{{cite book|last1=Schinder|first1=Scott|last2=Schwartz|first2=Andy|page=261|year=2008|title=Icons of Rock: An Encyclopedia of the Legends Who Changed Music Forever \u2013 Volume 1|publisher=Greenwood Press|location=Westport, CT|isbn=978-0-313-33845-8}}</ref>\n\nBy the time the original line-up of the Kentucky Colonels folded in late 1965, White had become a respected and well-known guitarist.<ref name=\"timeless\"/>  Abandoning bluegrass temporarily, he switched from his [[Martin D-28]] acoustic guitar to an electric [[Fender Telecaster]], with the intention of becoming a studio musician like his hero James Burton.<ref name=\"lightning\"/>  Transitioning to electric guitar required White to modify his right hand playing technique, switch from [[open chord]]ing to [[fret]]ting the whole [[Neck (music)|guitar neck]] with his left hand, and practice using the tone and volume controls.<ref name=\"lightning\"/>  However, he soon mastered the intricacies of the instrument and, between 1965 and 1968, he undertook session work for artists including [[Ricky Nelson]], [[the Monkees]], and the [[Vern Gosdin|Gosdin Brothers]].<ref name=\"timeless\"/><ref name=\"byrdwatcher2\"/> \n  \nAs 1965 turned into 1966, White met [[Gene Parsons]] and [[Gib Guilbeau]] at a recording session for the Gosdin Brothers and, shortly after, he began to perform live with the duo in local California clubs, as well as doing regular session work on their records, which were released under the moniker of Cajun Gib and Gene.<ref name=\"allmusic\"/><ref name=\"byrdwatcher2\"/>  1966 also saw White begin playing with a country group called Trio, which featured drummer Bart Haney and former Kentucky Colonel, Roger Bush, on bass.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher2\"/>  In autumn of that year, as a result of his friendship with Gilbeau, Parsons and the Gosdin Brothers, White was asked to provide [[lead guitar]] to ex-Byrd [[Gene Clark]]'s debut solo album, ''[[Gene Clark with the Gosdin Brothers]]''.<ref name=\"allmusic\"/><ref>{{cite book|last=Einarson|first=John|page=111|year=2005|title=Mr. Tambourine Man: The Life and Legacy of The Byrds' Gene Clark|publisher=Backbeat Books|location=San Francisco|isbn=0-87930-793-5}}</ref>  White briefly joined Clark's touring band shortly thereafter.<ref>{{cite book|last=Einarson|first=John|page=117|year=2005|title=Mr. Tambourine Man: The Life and Legacy of The Byrds' Gene Clark|publisher=Backbeat Books|location=San Francisco|isbn=0-87930-793-5}}</ref>\n\nDuring the Clark album sessions, White reconnected with mandolin player and [[bassist]] [[Chris Hillman]], who he had known during the early 1960s as a member of the bluegrass combo [[the Hillmen]].<ref name=\"hjort3\">{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|pages=113\u2013115|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  Hillman was currently a member of the Byrds and, in December 1966, he invited White to contribute countrified lead guitar playing to his songs \"Time Between\" and \"The Girl with No Name\", which both appeared on the Byrds' ''[[Younger Than Yesterday]]'' album.<ref name=\"hjort3\"/>  The country-oriented nature of the songs was something of a stylistic departure for the group and can be seen as an early indicator of the experimentation with country music that would color the Byrds' subsequent work.<ref>{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Younger Than Yesterday|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/lpyty.html|access-date=2009-08-04|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090428180437/http://ebni.com/byrds/lpyty.html|archive-date=2009-04-28}}</ref>  White also contributed guitar to the band's follow-up album, ''[[The Notorious Byrd Brothers]]'',<ref>{{cite book|last=Einarson|first=John|title=Desperados: The Roots of Country Rock|year=2001|publisher=Cooper Square Press|location=New York City|isbn=0-81541-065-4|page=[https://archive.org/details/desperadosrootso00eina/page/77 77]|url=https://archive.org/details/desperadosrootso00eina/page/77}}</ref> and to their seminal 1968 [[country rock]] release, ''[[Sweetheart of the Rodeo]]''.<ref name=\"allmusic3\">{{Cite web|url=http://www.allmusic.com/artist/nashville-west-mn0000316537/biography|title=Nashville West biography|last=Hage|first=Erik|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-15}}</ref>\n\n==Nashville West==\n{{main|Nashville West}}\n{{Listen |filename=Nashville_West_StringBender.ogg|title=Nashville West (a.k.a. Hong Kong Hillbilly)|description=An excerpt from a 1967 live recording of [[Nashville West]] performing their self-titled signature song.  Written by Clarence White and Gene Parsons, the song \"Nashville West\" provides an example of White's [[electric guitar]] playing technique.}}\nBy mid-1967, White had begun performing at night in the band the Reasons (a.k.a. [[Nashville West]]), which included bass player Wayne Moore, along with Parsons and Guilbeau (as banjoist-turned-drummer and lead singer respectively).<ref name=\"hjort4\">{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|pages=138\u2013139|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  The band mostly worked at the Nashville West club in El Monte, California, frequently borrowing the club's name as their own.<ref name=\"hjort4\"/>  Critic Erik Hage has said that, in the years since their formation, the band have become legendary as one of the first to play a seamless blend of country and rock,<ref name=\"allmusic3\"/> although L.A. group the [[International Submarine Band]], which featured country rock pioneer [[Gram Parsons]] (no relation to Gene), were also exploring a similar sound concurrently.<ref>{{cite book|last1=Erlewine|first1=Stephen Thomas|last2=Deming|first2=Mark|title=All Music Guide to Country|year=2003|edition=2nd|publisher=Backbeat Books|location=San Francisco|isbn=0-87930-760-9|pages=365\u2013366}}</ref>\n\nA live recording of Nashville West would eventually be released in 1979,<ref name=\"timeless2\"/> which music historian [[Richie Unterberger]] later described as being \"of considerable historical interest for anyone interested in the roots of country-rock\".<ref name=\"allmusic4\">{{Cite web|url={{AllMusic|class=album|id=nashville-west-mw0000416582|pure_url=yes}}|title=Nashville West album review|last=Unterberger|first=Richie|author-link=Richie Unterberger|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-15}}</ref>  Unterberger also remarked that the recording illustrated Nashville West as having \"more electric rock influences than most country acts were using at the time.\"<ref name=\"allmusic\"/>  In addition to being a member of Nashville West, White was also a member of another country bar band that regularly played at the Nashville West club called the Roustabouts.<ref name=\"lightning\"/>\n\nIn July 1967, White signed with [[Gary S. Paxton|Gary Paxton]]'s Bakersfield International record label and released a pair of solo [[Single (music)|singles]]: \"Tango for a Sad Mood\" b/w \"Tuff and Stringy\" and \"Grandma Funderbunks Music Box\" b/w \"Riff Raff\".<ref name=\"disc\"/><ref name=\"hjort4\"/>  He also reportedly recorded a solo album for the label, although it has never been released.<ref name=\"allmusic\"/>\n\n==The StringBender==\n{{main|B-Bender}}\n[[Image:1968 Parsons Bender fig 2-3-4.JPG|thumb|right|350px|A schematic of the first Parsons/White StringBender (a.k.a. the B-Bender)]]\nDuring 1967, while they were both members of Nashville West, White and Parsons invented a device that enabled Clarence to simulate the sound of a [[pedal steel guitar]] on his 1954 Fender Telecaster.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher3\">{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Clarence White: Nashville West|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/spnw1.html|access-date=2013-08-17|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20130730163655/http://ebni.com/byrds/spnw1.html|archive-date=2013-07-30}}</ref>  The need for such a device was driven by White's desire to bend his guitar's [[Strings (music)|B-string]] up a full [[Major second|tone]], while keeping his left hand on the strings and [[Fingerboard|fretboard]].<ref name=\"hjort5\">{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=169|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  In order to achieve this feat, White felt that he needed a third hand.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher3\"/>  The guitarist turned to his friend Parsons, who was an amateur [[machinist]], and asked him to design and build an apparatus to pull or drop the B-string.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher3\"/>\n\nThe device, which was known as the Parsons/White StringBender (also known as the [[B-Bender]]), was a spring-lever mechanism built into the inside of White's guitar, which linked to the guitar's strap button and the B-string.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/><ref name=\"hjort5\"/>  When it was activated, by pulling down on the guitar neck, it pulled on the B-string and caused the guitar to simulate the \"crying\" sound of a pedal steel.<ref name=\"Kingsbury\"/>  White would go on to use the device extensively as a member of the Byrds and, as a result, the distinctive sound of the StringBender would become a defining characteristic of that band's music during White's tenure with the group.<ref>{{cite book|last1=Fox|first1=Pamela|last2=Ching|first2=Barbara|page=210|year=2008|title=Old Roots, New Routes: The Cultural Politics of Alt. Country Music|publisher=University of Michigan Press|location=Michigan|isbn=978-0-472-05053-6}}</ref>\n\n==The Byrds==\n{{main|The Byrds}}\n{{Listen |filename=Byrds_Tulsa_County_Alt_Version.ogg|title=Tulsa County (alternate version)|description=An excerpt from an [[outtake]] version of the song \"Tulsa County\" by the Byrds. This audio clip highlights Clarence White's use of the [[B-Bender|StringBender]], which was fitted to his [[Fender Telecaster]] to enable him to simulate the sound of a [[pedal steel guitar]].}}\n[[File:ClarenceWhiteKralingen1970 (cropped).jpg|255px|thumb|Clarence White on stage with the Byrds at the [[Holland Pop Festival]] in Kralingen, Netherlands, 27 June 1970.]]\nFollowing the abrupt departure from the Byrds of singer and guitarist [[Gram Parsons]] in July 1968,<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|pages=178\u2013179|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref> White was invited to join the group as a full-time member. He remained until the band was finally dissolved by [[lead guitar]]ist [[Roger McGuinn]] in February 1973.  This extended tenure with the band makes White the second longest-serving member of the Byrds after McGuinn.<ref name=\"fricke\">{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Live at the Fillmore \u2013 February 1969]]|chapter=Knights of the Road|others=The Byrds|year=2000|last=Fricke|first=David|author-link=David Fricke|page=4|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Columbia Records|Columbia]]/[[Legacy Recordings|Legacy]]|location=New York City}}</ref>  White was brought into the group at [[Bassist|bass player]] Chris Hillman's suggestion, as someone who could handle the band's older rock material and their newer country-flavored repertoire.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher4\">{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Clarence White: With the Byrds and After, 1968-1973|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/memcw3.html|access-date=2013-08-13|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090413173313/http://ebni.com/byrds/memcw3.html|archive-date=2009-04-13}}</ref>\n\nOnce he was a member of the Byrds, White began to express dissatisfaction with the band's current drummer, [[Kevin Kelley (musician)|Kevin Kelley]].<ref name=\"timeless3\">{{Cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|pages=276\u2013277|year=1998|title=The Byrds: Timeless Flight Revisited - The Sequel|edition=2nd|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=0-9529540-1-X}}</ref>  Before long, he had persuaded McGuinn and Hillman to replace Kelley with his friend from the recently dissolved Nashville West, [[Gene Parsons]] (no relation to Gram).<ref name=\"timeless3\"/>\n\nHillman quit the Byrds within a month of White joining, in order to form [[the Flying Burrito Brothers]] with Gram Parsons.<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|pages=189\u2013190|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  At around this same time, White and Gene Parsons undertook some informal rehearsing and recording with Hillman and Gram Parsons, as part of a prototype version of the Burrito Brothers.<ref name=\"icons\">{{cite book|last1=Schinder|first1=Scott|last2=Schwartz|first2=Andy|pages=276\u2013277|year=2008|title=Icons of Rock: An Encyclopedia of the Legends Who Changed Music Forever \u2013 Volume 1|publisher=Greenwood Press|location=Westport, CT|isbn=978-0-313-33845-8}}</ref>  However, the pair declined an invitation to join the new country rock group and instead opted to stay with McGuinn's new-look Byrds.<ref name=\"icons\"/>\n\n{{quote box|width=23em|\"The greatest thing about Clarence was that he never played anything that sounded vaguely weak ... he was always driving\u2014''into'' the music\u2014and that pulled the whole band up. He had that conservative thing he got from Bluegrass, where you underplay it on stage, where everybody poker-faces it. He would do these truly outrageous things on guitar, but hardly move a muscle, aside from his hands.\"|\u2014Byrds' leader [[Roger McGuinn]] recalling White's on-stage musicianship and demeanor.<ref name=\"fricke\"/>}}\nThe White-era version of the Byrds, featuring McGuinn, White and Parsons, along with bassists [[John York (musician)|John York]] (September 1968\u2013September 1969) and [[Skip Battin]] (September 1969\u2013February 1973), released five albums and toured relentlessly between 1969 and 1972.  [[Journalist]] Steve Leggett has noted that, although the original line-up of the Byrds gets the most attention and praise, the latter-day version\u2014featuring McGuinn and White's dual lead guitar work\u2014was regarded by critics and audiences as much more accomplished in concert than any previous configuration of the band had been.<ref>{{cite web|last=Leggitt|first=Steve|title=Live at Royal Albert Hall 1971 album review|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|url=http://www.allmusic.com/album/live-at-royal-albert-hall-1971-r1364393/review|access-date=2013-08-18}}</ref>  Similarly, authors Scott Schinder and Andy Schwartz have commented that although the White-era Byrds failed to achieve the commercial success of the original line-up, the group were a formidable live act and a consistently in-demand attraction on the touring circuit.<ref name=\"icons\"/>  The authors also cited the Byrds' archival release ''[[Live at the Fillmore \u2013 February 1969]]'' as a good example of the White-era band's musical potency.<ref name=\"icons\"/>  ''[[Rolling Stone]]'' journalist [[David Fricke]] has commented on White's contribution to the band by noting, \"with his powerful, impeccable tone and melodic ingenuity, White did much to rebuild the creative reputation of the Byrds and define the road-hearty sound of the group at the turn of the '70s.\"<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Dr. Byrds & Mr. Hyde]]|chapter=McGuinn Takes the Reins|others=The Byrds|year=1997|last=Fricke|first=David|page=5|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Columbia Records|Columbia]]/[[Legacy Recordings|Legacy]]|location=New York City}}</ref>\n\nThe first Byrds' album to feature White as a full member was ''[[Dr. Byrds & Mr. Hyde]]'', which was released in early 1969.<ref name=\"allmusic5\">{{Cite web|url={{AllMusic|class=album|id=mw0000264578|pure_url=yes}}|title=Dr. Byrds and Mr. Hyde album review|last=Deming|first=Mark|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-23}}</ref>  The album included a re-recording of the Gene Parsons and White-penned instrumental \"Nashville West\",<ref name=\"allmusic5\"/> as well as a rendition of the [[Traditional music|traditional]] song \"[[Old Blue (song)|Old Blue]]\", which was the first Byrds' recording to utilize the StringBender.<ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|author-link=Johnny Rogan|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|page=499}}</ref>  The ''[[Ballad of Easy Rider (album)|Ballad of Easy Rider]]'' album followed in November 1969, on which White could be heard leading the band through a rendition of the traditional song \"[[Oil in My Lamp]]\", representing the guitarist's first lead vocal performance as a Byrd.<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Ballad of Easy Rider (album)|Ballad of Easy Rider]]|chapter=After the Revolution: The Byrds' Great Escape|others=The Byrds|year=1997|last=Fricke|first=David|page=3|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Columbia Records|Columbia]]/[[Legacy Recordings|Legacy]]|location=New York City}}</ref>\n\n1970 saw the Byrds release the [[double album]] ''[[Untitled (The Byrds album)|(Untitled)]]'', which consisted of one [[LP album|LP]] of [[Rock concert|live concert]] recordings and another of new [[studio recording]]s.<ref>{{cite book|last=Plasketes|first=George|title=B-sides, Undercurrents and Overtones: Peripheries to Popular in Music, 1960 to the Present|url=https://archive.org/details/bsidesundercurre00plas|url-access=limited|year=2009|publisher=Ashgate Publishing Ltd.|location=Farnham, UK|isbn=978-0-7546-6561-8|page=[https://archive.org/details/bsidesundercurre00plas/page/n30 18]}}</ref>  Upon release, the album was a critical and commercial success on both sides of the Atlantic,<ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|page=539}}</ref> peaking at number 40 on the [[Billboard 200|''Billboard'' Top LPs]] chart and reaching number 11 on the [[UK Albums Chart]].<ref>{{cite book|last=Whitburn|first=Joel|page=[https://archive.org/details/joelwhitburnstop00whitbu/page/121 121]|year=2002|title=Top Pop Albums 1955-2001|publisher=Record Research Inc.|location=Menomonee Falls, WI|isbn=0-89820-147-0|url=https://archive.org/details/joelwhitburnstop00whitbu/page/121}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|last=Brown|first=Tony|title=The Complete Book of the British Charts|year=2000|publisher=Omnibus Press|location=London|isbn=0-7119-7670-8|page=130}}</ref>  Two of the album's studio recordings featured White singing lead vocals: a [[cover version]] of the [[Lowell George]] composition \"Truck Stop Girl\" and a rendition of [[Huddie Ledbetter|Leadbelly]]'s \"[[Take a Whiff on Me]]\" (the latter also featured White playing mandolin).<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=243|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  In addition, excerpts from an instrumental [[Jam session|jam]], recorded during the ''(Untitled)'' album sessions and logged in the [[Columbia Records]]' files under the title of \"Fifteen Minute Jam\", were later released as \"White's Lightning\" and \"White's Lightning Pt.2\" on ''[[The Byrds (box set)|The Byrds]]'' [[box set]] and the [[remaster]]ed double [[Compact Disc|CD]] version of ''(Untitled)'' respectively.<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[The Byrds (box set)|The Byrds]]|others=The Byrds|year=1990|last=Irwin|first=Bob|page=10|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Columbia Records|Columbia]]/[[Legacy Recordings|Legacy]]|location=New York City}}</ref><ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Untitled (The Byrds album)|(Untitled)/(Unissued)]]|others=The Byrds|year=2000|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|page=13|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Columbia Records|Columbia]]/[[Legacy Recordings|Legacy]]|location=New York City}}</ref>\n\n[[File:The Byrds (1970).jpg|255px|thumb|Clarence White (third from left) on tour with the Byrds in the Netherlands, June 1970.]]\nThe 1971 ''[[Byrdmaniax]]'' album saw White singing lead vocals on \"My Destiny\", written by [[Helen Carter]],<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=264|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref> and \"[[Jamaica Say You Will (song)|Jamaica Say You Will]]\", penned by the then little-known [[songwriter]] [[Jackson Browne]].<ref>{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Byrdmaniax|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/lpbmx.html|access-date=2013-08-23|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20090529071447/http://ebni.com/byrds/lpbmx.html|archive-date=2009-05-29}}</ref>  In addition, White received a co-writing credit for the album's bluegrass instrumental \"Green Apple Quick Step\".<ref>{{Cite web|url={{AllMusic|class=album|id=mw0000690219|pure_url=yes}}|title=Byrdmaniax album review|last=Deming|first=Mark|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-23}}</ref>  This song also featured White's father, Eric White Sr., on [[harmonica]].<ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|page=571}}</ref>\n\n''[[Farther Along (The Byrds album)|Farther Along]]'', released in November 1971, would prove to be the final album by the White-era Byrds.<ref name=\"fricke2\">{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Farther Along (The Byrds album)|Farther Along]]|chapter=Farther Along: The Byrds at Twilight|others=The Byrds|year=2000|last=Fricke|first=David|pages=4\u20135|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Columbia Records|Columbia]]/[[Legacy Recordings|Legacy]]|location=New York City}}</ref>  It featured White singing the [[Gospel music|Gospel]] [[hymn]] and title track \"[[Farther Along (song)|Farther Along]]\" and a cover of the Larry Murray composition \"Bugler\".<ref name=\"fricke2\"/>  This latter song features White playing mandolin and has been described by Byrds expert Tim Connors as, \"the best song on the album, and by far the best vocal ever recorded by Clarence White during his time with the Byrds.\"<ref>{{cite web|last=Connors|first=Tim|title=Farther Along|publisher=Euclid Business Network|website=ByrdWatcher: A Field Guide to the Byrds of Los Angeles|url=http://ebni.com/byrds/lpfa.html|access-date=2013-08-23|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110125071735/http://ebni.com/byrds/lpfa.html|archive-date=2011-01-25}}</ref>\n\nFollowing the release of ''Farther Along'', the band continued to tour throughout 1972,<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=293|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref> but no new Byrds album appeared.<ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|page=590}}</ref>  In late 1972, the original five-piece line-up of the Byrds reunited and, as a result, McGuinn decided to disband the existing version of the band.  Parsons had been fired in July 1972 and Battin was dismissed by McGuinn in early 1973.<ref name=\"fricke2\"/>  The last concert by the White-era version of the Byrds (which at this point featured former Byrd Chris Hillman on bass and [[Joe Lala]] on drums) was given on February 24, 1973, at The Capitol Theatre, Passaic, New Jersey, with White and McGuinn jokingly firing each other from the band afterwards.<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=316|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|pages=610\u2013611}}</ref>\n\nDespite being on tour or in the recording studio with the Byrds for the majority of the time between 1969 and 1972, White continued to undertake selected session work for other recording artists.  During this period he played on [[Joe Cocker]]'s 1969 album ''[[Joe Cocker!]]'', [[Randy Newman]]'s 1970 album ''[[12 Songs (Randy Newman album)|12 Songs]]'', and [[the Everly Brothers]]' ''[[Stories We Could Tell]]'' from 1972.<ref name=\"allmusic\"/>  In early 1971, White also contributed guitar to [[Paul Siebel]]'s ''[[Jack-Knife Gypsy]]'' album and the title track of the ''L.A. Getaway'' album by Joel Scott-Hill, [[John Barbata]] and [[Chris Ethridge]].<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=266|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  Other albums that White contributed his guitar playing to while he was a member of the Byrds include [[Linda Ronstadt]]'s ''[[Hand Sown ... Home Grown]]'' (1969), [[Rita Coolidge]]'s ''[[Rita Coolidge (album)|Rita Coolidge]]'' (1971), [[Marc Benno]]'s ''Minnows'' (1971), [[Jackson Browne]]'s ''[[Jackson Browne (album)|Jackson Browne]]'' (1972), Gene Clark's ''[[Roadmaster (album)|Roadmaster]]'' (1973), and a trio of [[Arlo Guthrie]] albums: ''[[Running Down the Road]]'' (1969), ''[[Washington County (album)|Washington County]]'' (1970) and ''[[Hobo's Lullaby (album)|Hobo's Lullaby]]'' (1972).<ref name=\"byrdwatcher4\"/>\n\n==Post-Byrds==\n{{quote box|width=23em|\"Clarence was at my birthday party the night before, and he and I had agreed to start working together again\u2014not necessarily as the Byrds, but in some form. He was incredibly talented, and full of life and full of music. I know he'd still be doing something great today if he was still around.\"|\u2014Roger McGuinn recalling a meeting with White on the day before he died.<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Live at Royal Albert Hall 1971]]|others=The Byrds|year=2008|first=Scott|last=Schinder|page=5|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Sundazed Records]]|location=Coxsackie, NY}}</ref>}}\nIn mid-February 1973, just prior to the break up of the White-era version of the Byrds, White joined with guitarist [[Peter Rowan]], mandolinist [[David Grisman]], fiddler [[Richard Greene (fiddle player)|Richard Greene]], and banjoist [[Bill Keith (musician)|Bill Keith]] to form the bluegrass [[Supergroup (music)|supergroup]] [[Muleskinner (band)|Muleskinner]].<ref>{{Cite book|last=Hjort|first=Christopher|page=313|year=2008|title=So You Want To Be A Rock 'n' Roll Star: The Byrds Day-By-Day (1965\u20131973)|publisher=Jawbone Press|location=London|isbn=978-1-906002-15-2}}</ref>  The musicians initially assembled as a one-off [[Pickup group|pickup band]] to back bluegrass pioneer [[Bill Monroe]] for a television program, but ended up performing on their own when Monroe's tour bus broke down on the way to the television studios.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher4\"/>  A recording of this broadcast, which was once thought lost, was released as an album in 1992, under the title ''[[Muleskinner Live: Original Television Soundtrack|Muleskinner Live]]''.<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Muleskinner Live: Original Television Soundtrack]]|others=[[Muleskinner (band)|Muleskinner]]|year=2003|last=Koon|first=William Henry|page=2|type=CD booklet|publisher=Sierra Records|location=Pasadena, CA}}</ref><ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|page=584|year=1998|title=The Byrds: Timeless Flight Revisited\u2013The Sequel|edition=2nd|publisher=Rogan House|isbn=0-9529540-1-X}}</ref>  A [[VHS|VHS video cassette]] of the broadcast was also released in 1992 and later re-issued on [[DVD]].\n\nAs a result of the success of their appearance on the television broadcast, the band was offered a one album [[recording contract]] with [[Warner Bros. Records]].<ref>{{cite web|last=Kurutz|first=Steve|title=Muleskinner biography|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|url=http://www.allmusic.com/artist/muleskinner-mn0000511177/biography|access-date=2013-11-03}}</ref>  Sessions for the album took place at the [[Record Plant]] in Los Angeles between March 27 and April 14, 1973, with Richard Greene and [[Joe Boyd]] [[Record producer|producing]].<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Muleskinner (album)|A Potpourri of Bluegrass Jam]]|others=[[Muleskinner (band)|Muleskinner]]|year=1994|type=CD booklet|publisher=Sierra Records|location=Pasadena, CA}}</ref>  The music the band recorded for the ''[[Muleskinner (album)|Muleskinner]]'' album (a.k.a. ''A Potpourri of Bluegrass Jam'') was in the vein of [[country rock]], [[traditional bluegrass]] and [[progressive bluegrass]] (or \"newgrass\").<ref name=\"allmusic6\">{{cite web|last=Chadbourne|first=Eugene|author-link=Eugene Chadbourne|title=Muleskinner album review|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|url=http://www.allmusic.com/album/muleskinner-mw0000868409|access-date=2013-11-04}}</ref>  It was also one of the first bluegrass albums to feature a full [[drum kit]].<ref name=\"allmusic6\"/>  The album was released in the latter half of 1973 and is nowadays regarded by critics as a milestone in the development of progressive bluegrass, with ex-band members Greene, Keith, Grisman, and Rowan all going on to become important figures in the development of that genre.<ref name=\"byrdwatcher4\"/>\n\nIn addition to his work with Muleskinner, White also undertook a number of sessions between late 1972 and early 1973 for his friend Gene Parsons' debut solo album ''[[Kindling (album)|Kindling]]''.<ref name=\"parsons\">{{cite AV media notes|title=[[Kindling (album)|Kindling]]|others=[[Gene Parsons]]|year=1994|last=Parsons|first=Gene|author-link=Gene Parsons|pages=4\u20136|type=CD booklet|publisher=Sierra Records|location=Los Angeles}}</ref>  White's distinctive guitar and mandolin playing can be heard on the tracks \"Do Not Disturb\", \"On the Spot\", \"Sonic Bummer\", \"I Must Be a Tree\", \"Banjo Dog\", \"Back Again\", and \"Drunkard's Dream\" (the latter of which also features White contributing harmony vocals).<ref name=\"parsons\"/>\n\nFollowing completion of the ''Muleskinner'' album in April 1973, White reunited with his brothers Roland and Eric Jr. for a tour as the White Brothers (a.k.a. The New Kentucky Colonels).<ref name=\"byrdwatcher4\"/>  After playing a handful of shows in California, the White Brothers departed for Europe in May 1973.<ref>{{Cite web|url=http://www.smokemusicarchive.com/content/roland-white-solo-record|title=A Record of His Own: Roland White's Solo Reissued|last=Moses|first=Bob|website=Smoke Music Archive|publisher=Smoke Creative|access-date=2014-03-03|url-status=dead|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20151004101904/http://www.smokemusicarchive.com/content/roland-white-solo-record|archive-date=2015-10-04}}</ref>\n\nReturning to the U.S., White's final bout of touring took place with the New Kentucky Colonels in June 1973, as part of a four-date country rock package tour with Gram Parsons, [[Emmylou Harris]], [[Country Gazette (band)|Country Gazette]], [[Sneaky Pete Kleinow]], Gene Parsons, [[Byron Berline]], and [[Chris Ethridge]] among others.<ref>{{cite book|last=Meyer|first=David N.|title=Twenty Thousand Roads: The Ballad of Gram Parsons and His Cosmic American Music|year=2007|publisher=Villard Books|location=New York, NY|isbn=978-0-375-50570-6|pages=[https://archive.org/details/twentythousandro00meye/page/404 404\u2013405]|url=https://archive.org/details/twentythousandro00meye/page/404}}</ref>  Although Gram and Clarence had been acquainted with one another since the Byrds' ''Sweetheart of the Rodeo'' sessions, the pair would develop a fast friendship during the mini-tour, after a very acrimonious re-acquaintance.<ref>{{cite book|last=Fong-Torres|first=Ben|author-link=Ben Fong-Torres|title=Hickory Wind: The Life and Times of Gram Parsons|year=1998|publisher=[[St. Martin's Griffin]]|location=New York, NY|isbn=978-0-312-19464-2}}</ref>  Following the end of the package tour, White entered the recording studio with producer Jim Dickson on June 28 and 29, 1973 to begin work on a solo album.<ref name=\"silver\">{{cite AV media notes|title=Silver Meteor: A Progressive Country Anthology|others=Various artists|year=1980|last=Koon|first=William Henry|type=LP sleeve|publisher=Sierra Records|location=Los Angeles}}</ref>  He recorded a total of six songs,<ref>{{cite book|last=Rogan|first=Johnny|title=Requiem for the Timeless: Volume 1|year=2011|publisher=Rogan House|location=London|isbn=978-0-95295-408-8|pages=625\u2013626}}</ref> four of which would belatedly be released on the archival album ''Silver Meteor: A Progressive Country Anthology'' in 1980.<ref name=\"silver\"/>\n\n==Death==\nWhite died on July 15, 1973, after being struck by a drunk driver.<ref name=\"hjort6\"/><ref>\"Car kills Topanga musician\", ''Long Beach (CA) Press-Telegram'', Monday, July 16, 1973, p. 13 (A Topanga musician loading instruments aboard his van was struck and killed Sunday...\")</ref> The accident occurred shortly after 2 a.m., while he and his brother Roland were loading equipment into their car in Palmdale, California, following a White Brothers concert. Gram Parsons was especially shaken by his death; he led a singalong of \"[[Farther Along (song)|Farther Along]]\" at White's funeral service and later conceived his final song (before his own death), \"In My Hour of Darkness\", as a partial tribute to White.{{cn|date=July 2024}}\n\n==Musical influence==\nClarence White helped popularize the acoustic guitar as a lead instrument in bluegrass music, building on the work of guitarists such as Doc Watson. Prior to the advent of the more aggressive flatpicking style pioneered by guitarists like Watson and White, the guitar was strictly a rhythm instrument, save for a few exceptions (such as the occasional guitar track by banjoist [[Don Reno]]). Many of the most influential flatpickers of the 20th century cite White as a primary influence, including [[Dan Crary]], [[Norman Blake (American musician)|Norman Blake]], and [[Tony Rice]].  Rice owned and played White's highly modified 1935 [[C.F. Martin & Company|Martin]] D-28. [[David Grier]] and [[Russ Barenberg]] are two other acoustic guitarists who were heavily influenced by White's guitar work.  White's bluegrass playing with the Kentucky Colonels was also a considerable influence on [[Jerry Garcia]] of the [[Grateful Dead]],<ref name=\"lightning\"/> who traveled with the band during 1964.<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=Livin' in the Past|others=[[Kentucky Colonels]]|year=2003|first=John|last=Delgatto|page=2|type=CD booklet|publisher=Sierra Records|location=Pasadena}}</ref>\n\nOn the electric side of the guitar spectrum, White was similarly influential.{{Citation needed|date=January 2008}} Together with fellow Byrds bandmember [[Gene Parsons]], White invented the [[B-Bender]] device. This device raises the B-string (second string) of the guitar a whole step by the use of pulleys and levers attached to both the upper strap knob and the second string on the guitar. It is activated by pushing down on the neck, and produces a \"[[pedal steel]]\" type sound. [[Arlen Roth]], heavily influenced by this style, did not at the time know that White and Parsons had invented a B-bender, so instead developed his own unique all-finger bending version of this technique. This was heavily documented in his ground-breaking book, \"Nashville Guitar\", all of his recordings, as well as his book \"Masters of the Telecaster\". Subsequently, his [[Telecaster]] sound became as notable as his bluegrass playing. [[Marty Stuart]], another guitarist influenced by White's playing, now owns and regularly plays White's 1954 [[Fender Telecaster]] with the prototype B-Bender.<ref>[http://www.martystuart.com/ZArt-GuitarPlayer8-94.htm] Gill, Chris: \"Steel And Wood, Heart And Soul - The Marty Stuart Guitar Collection,\" reprinted from ''Guitar Player Magazine'', August 1994.</ref>\n\nMusic archivist and writer [[Alec Palao]] has called White \"one of a handful of true greats amongst the instrumentalists of 20th century popular music\", before adding that \"the waves created by the guitarist's idiosyncratic style are still forming ripples within bluegrass, country and rock 'n' roll.\"<ref>{{cite AV media notes|title=Tuff & Stringy: Sessions 1966-68|chapter=Part One: Sometimes the Journey Can Be as Interesting as the Destination|others=Clarence White|year=2003|last=Palao|first=Alec|page=4|type=CD booklet|publisher=[[Ace Records (United Kingdom)|Ace Records]]|location=London}}</ref>  In 2003, White was ranked No.&nbsp;41 on ''[[Rolling Stone]]'' magazine's list of the 100 Greatest Guitarists of All Time. In 2010, guitar manufacturer [[Gibson Guitar Corporation|Gibson]] ranked White at No.&nbsp;42 on their Top 50 Guitarists of All Time list.<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.gibson.com/en-us/Lifestyle/Features/Top-50-Guitarists-524/|title=Gibson.com Top 50 Guitarists of All Time \u2013 50 to 41|publisher=Gibson.com|access-date=2010-06-03|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20100530105529/http://www.gibson.com/en-us/Lifestyle/Features/Top-50-Guitarists-524/|archive-date=2010-05-30|url-status=dead}}</ref>\n\n==Selected album discography==\nNOTES:\n* This discography does not include albums that Clarence White played on as a session musician, with the exception of ''Dobro Country'', on which he is billed by name.\n*Sources for this section are [[Johnny Rogan]]'s book ''Timeless Flight Revisited'' and the Kentucky Colonels discography at the [[AllMusic]] website.<ref name=\"timeless2\"/><ref>{{Cite web|url=http://www.allmusic.com/artist/the-kentucky-colonels-mn0000083453/discography|title=Kentucky Colonels discography|website=[[Allmusic]]|publisher=[[Rovi Corp]]|access-date=2013-08-14}}</ref>\n\n===Kentucky Colonels===\n* ''The New Sound of Bluegrass America'' (1963)\n* ''[[Appalachian Swing!]]'' (1964)\n* ''Kentucky Colonels'' (1974) &mdash; https://www.billystrings.com\n| associated_acts   = {{flatlist|\n*[[Don Julin]]\n*[[Billy Failing]]\n*[[Royal Masat]]\n*[[Jarrod Walker]]\n*[[John Mailander]]\n*[[Brad Tucker]]\n*[[Drew Mautlich]]\n*[[Molly Tuttle]]\n*[[Sierra Ferrell]]\n*[[Brendino Schlongergan]]\n*[[Lindsay Lou]]\n*[[Green Shirt Guy]]\n*[[Billy and the Kids]]\n*[[B\u00e9la Fleck]]\n*[[Jonah Calvo]]\n| website         = {{oweb|http://www.billystrings.com}}\n}}\n}}\n\n'''Billy Strings''' (born '''William Lee Apostol''', October 3, 1992) is an American guitarist and [[Bluegrass music|bluegrass]] musician.<ref name=\"rolling stone\">{{Cite magazine|url=https://www.rollingstone.com/country/lists/10-new-country-artists-you-need-to-know-august-2017-w496530/billy-strings-w496534|title=10 New Country Artists You Need to Know: August 2017|magazine=[[Rolling Stone (magazine)|Rolling Stone]]|date=August 8, 2017|access-date=January 31, 2018}}</ref> His album [[Home (Billy Strings album)|''Home'']] won the [[Grammy Award for Best Bluegrass Album]] in 2021.\n\n==Early life==\nBilly Strings was born William Lee Apostol on October 3, 1992, in [[Lansing, Michigan]]. His father died of a heroin overdose when he was two and his mother remarried Terry Barber, an accomplished amateur bluegrass musician whom Strings regards as his father. The family later moved to [[Morehead, Kentucky]], and then to [[Muir, Michigan]]. While he was still a preteen, his parents became addicted to methamphetamine. He left the family home at the age of 13 and went through a period of hard drug usage. His family eventually achieved sobriety; Billy stopped using hard drugs and drinking alcohol, becoming [[California sober|\"California sober\"]] and consuming only cannabis and similar \"light drugs\" (ie psychedelics).<ref name=\"RollingStoneJune2022\">{{cite news |last1=Hudak |first1=Joseph |title=Drugs, Bluegrass, and Bass Fishing: How Billy Strings Outran His Past and Infiltrated the Mainstream |url=https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-features/billy-strings-bluegrass-fishing-concert-1369241/ |access-date=25 August 2022 |publisher=Rolling Stone |date=June 22, 2022}}</ref><ref name=\"northernexpress.com\">{{cite web|url=https://www.northernexpress.com/news/feature/article-5963-the-amazing-billy-strings/|title=The Amazing Billy Strings|publisher=Northern Express}}</ref> \n\nBarber was a heavy influence on his stepson, introducing him to traditional bluegrass artists at a young age,<ref name=\"rolling stone\"/> including [[Doc Watson]], [[Del McCoury]], [[David Grisman]],<ref name=\"Live for Live Music billy-strings\">{{cite web|url=https://liveforlivemusic.com/features/billy-strings-garcia-grisman/|title=Watch Billy Strings Crush Garcia & Grisman's \"Dreadful Wind & Rain\"|website=liveforlivemusic.com|date=March 23, 2018}}</ref> [[Bill Monroe]], [[John Hartford]], [[Ralph Stanley]], [[Earl Scruggs]] and [[Larry Sparks]].<ref>{{cite web|url=http://www.midwesterngentleman.com/music/keep-stringin-the-billy-strings-interview/|title=Midwestern Gentleman - Keep Stringin': The Billy Strings Interview|date=December 8, 2019 |publisher=}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=http://wglt.org/post/billy-strings-just-beginning-already-wild-ride#stream/0|title=Billy Strings: Just Beginning An Already Wild Ride|publisher=[[NPR]]|access-date=November 7, 2018|archive-url=https://web.archive.org/web/20110519173408/http://http/|archive-date=May 19, 2011|url-status=dead}}</ref> Strings is also a [[Rock music|rock]] and [[Heavy metal music|metal]] fan, influenced by [[Jimi Hendrix]], [[Johnny Winter]], [[Widespread Panic]], [[Phish]], the [[Grateful Dead]], [[Def Leppard]], [[Between the Buried and Me]] and [[Black Sabbath]], and played in hard rock and indie rock bands in his teens.<ref name=\"northernexpress.com\"/><ref name=\"The Bluegrass Situation\">{{cite web|url=https://thebluegrasssituation.com/read/jumping-into-the-deep-end-a-conversation-with-billy-strings|title=JUMPING INTO THE DEEP END: A CONVERSATION WITH BILLY STRINGS|date=October 18, 2017 |publisher=The Bluegrass Situation}}</ref>\n\nApostol got his stage name, Billy Strings, from his aunt, who saw his ability on multiple traditional bluegrass instruments.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://greenvillejournal.com/2017/05/04/billy-strings-fascinatingly-unpredictable-performer/ |title=Billy Strings Is A Fascinatingly Unpredictable Performer|date=May 4, 2017 |publisher=Greenville Journal}}</ref>\n\n== Career ==\n\n=== Beginnings and ''Turmoil'' & ''Tinfoil'' (2012-2019) ===\n[[File:111219 RelixPressDayNYC BillyStrings JFaatzMedia-2910.jpg|left|thumb|300x300px|Billy Strings in 2019]]\nIn 2012, Don Julin, a mandolin player from [[Traverse City, Michigan]], and author of ''Mandolin for Dummies'', asked Strings to join him on a paying gig. The partnership lasted for the next four years. \n\n''[[Rolling Stone (magazine)|Rolling Stone]]'' named Strings one of the Top Ten New Country Artists to Know in 2017.<ref name=\"rolling stone\" /> On February 12, 2018, ''Rolling Stone'' published an article titled \"Bluegrass Prodigy Billy Strings Plots 2018 Spring Tour\", saying: \"Billy Strings doesn't have any trouble living up to his name. [He is] one of the latest breakneck guitar pickers to emerge in the bluegrass world.\"<ref name=\"Rolling Stone 2\">{{cite web|url=https://www.rollingstone.com/country/news/bluegrass-prodigy-billy-strings-plots-2018-spring-tour-w516633/|title=Bluegrass Prodigy Billy Strings Plots 2018 Spring Tour|magazine=[[Rolling Stone (magazine)|Rolling Stone]]|date=February 12, 2018 }}</ref> The [[International Bluegrass Music Association]] awarded him with the Momentum Instrumentalist of the Year award in 2016.<ref name=\"crossovertouring.com\">{{cite web |url=http://crossovertouring.com/billy-strings/|title=Crossover Touring|website=crossovertouring.com}}</ref> ''Bluegrass Situation'' named him a scene tastemaker in 2016.<ref name=\"crossovertouring.com\"/> Lisa Snedeker of ''[[HuffPost]]'' proclaimed ''Turmoil & Tinfoil'' as one of the best albums of 2017, writing: \"In September it charted at No. 3 on the Billboard Bluegrass charts. 'Nuff said.\"<ref name=\"huffingtonpost.com\">{{cite web |url=https://www.huffingtonpost.com/entry/year-in-review-favorite-musical-picks-for-2017_us_5a49b486e4b0d86c803c7835/|title=Year in Review: Favorite musical picks for 2017|date=January 2018 |publisher=Huffington Post}}</ref> In March 2018, ''Rolling Stone'' released Strings' debut music video for his song \"Dealing Despair\" from his album ''Turmoil & Tinfoil''.<ref name=\"rollingstone\">{{cite web|url=https://www.rollingstone.com/country/news/billy-strings-dealing-despair-see-intense-new-video-w517819/|title=See Bluegrass Shredder Billy Strings Call Out Violent Culture in 'Dealing Despair' Video |date=March 13, 2018 |magazine=[[Rolling Stone (magazine)|Rolling Stone]]}}</ref> He planned to play over 200 shows in 2018.<ref name=\"Daily Progress\">{{cite web|url=http://www.dailyprogress.com/steeped-in-versatility-billy-strings-keeps-returning-to-his-bluegrass/article_5abb6646-4e32-11e8-a8d8-1fa4895a60c8.html/|title=Steeped in versatility, Billy Strings Keeps Returning to His Bluegrass Roots|date=May 2, 2018 |publisher=Daily Progress}}</ref>\n\nHe has been invited to play on stage with artists including [[Phish]], [[Dierks Bentley]],<ref name=\"WMOT Roots Radio\">{{cite web|url=http://wmot.org/post/wmot-fundraiser-evening-dierks-bentley#stream/0|title=WMOT Fundraiser: An Evening with Dierks Bentley|website=wmot.org|date=March 6, 2018 }}</ref> [[Del McCoury]], [[Bill Kreutzmann]], [[Bob Weir]], [[David Grisman]], [[Larry Keel]], [[Sam Bush]],<ref name=\"Jambands.com\">{{cite web|url=http://www.jambands.com/news/2018/01/21/greensky-bluegrass-collaborate-with-sam-bush-and-billy-strings-in-nashville|title=Greensky Bluegrass Collaborate with Sam Bush and Billy Strings in Nashville|website=Jambands.com|date=January 21, 2018 }}</ref> [[The Marcus King Band]],<ref name=\"JamBase\">{{cite web|url=https://www.jambase.com/article/marcus-king-billy-strings-team-kings-strings-set-rooster-walk|title=Marcus King & Billy Strings To Team For 'King & Strings' Set At Rooster Walk|publisher=Jambase.com}}</ref> [[Greensky Bluegrass]], [[The Infamous Stringdusters]],<ref name=\"Live for Live Music aiken-bluegrass\">{{cite web |url=https://liveforlivemusic.com/features/aiken-bluegrass-festival-2017/|title=The Infamous Stringdusters, Fruition, & More To Bring The Pickin' To Aiken Bluegrass Festival |website=liveforlivemusic.com|date=April 13, 2017 }}</ref> [[The String Cheese Incident]], [[Leftover Salmon]], [[Widespread Panic]], [[Tool (band)|Tool]], and more. He has performed at festivals including Frankfort Bluegrass Festival (Frankfort, IL), Hookahville, Blue Ox, Pickathon, [[Merlefest]], DelFest, [[High Sierra Music Festival]], [[Lollapalooza]], [[Grey Fox Bluegrass Festival]], [[Telluride Bluegrass Festival]], John Hartford Memorial Festival,<ref>{{cite web|title = John Hartford Memorial Festival - Previous Lineup History| url = https://hartfordfest.com/previous-lineup-history| website = hartfordfest.com| access-date = June 2, 2018 }}</ref> Aiken Bluegrass Festival, [[Appaloosa Music Festival]], [[Wheatland Music Festival]], Red Wing Roots Music Festival, Bristol Rhythm & Roots, Rooster Walk, French Broad River Festival, Makers Trail Festival, Under the Big Sky Festival and the All Good Presents 4848 Festival. At Grey Fox, he served as their very first artist in residence in 2017 and 2018.<ref name=\"Delfest\">{{cite web|url=https://www.Delfest.com/lineup/|title=Lineup|website=delfest.com|date=November 15, 2021 }}</ref><ref name=\"Grey Fox Bluegrass Festival\">{{cite web|url=https://greyfoxbluegrass.com/festival/2018-lineup/|title=2018 Lineup|website=greyfoxbluegrass.com}}</ref> Strings has appeared on famous [[PBS]] musical TV programs ''[[Austin City Limits]]'' and ''[[Bluegrass Underground]]''.<ref name=\"NY Post\">{{cite web|url=https://nypost.com/2018/03/21/cave-concert-venue-gives-new-meaning-to-underground-music-scene/|title=Cave Concert Venue Gives New Meaning to Underground Music Scene|date=March 21, 2018 |publisher=NY Post}}</ref> He has toured with [[Greensky Bluegrass]], [[The Infamous Stringdusters]], [[Leftover Salmon]], {{proper name|[[I'm With Her (band)|I'm With Her]]}}, Cabinet and others.<ref name=\"crossovertouring.com\"/><ref name=\"jambase.com\">{{cite web|url=https://www.jambase.com/article/greensky-bluegrass-billy-strings-take-manhattan-recap-photos-videos/|title=Greensky Bluegrass & Billy Strings Take Manhattan|publisher=Jambase}}</ref> Of those bands he played with early in his career, he has cited Greensky Bluegrass specifically as a major influence and his \"favorite band.\"<ref name=\"Marquee Magazine\">{{cite web|url=http://marqueemag.com/2018/02/billy-strings/|title=Billy Strings breaks away from his mentor and is set to take his new band to the 'stratosphere'| date=February 1, 2018 | publisher=Marquee Magazine}}</ref>\n[[File:Billy Strings by Jesse Faatz 2023.jpg|thumb|300x300px|Billy Strings in 2023]]\nIn February 2017, Strings was named one of the six new rising stars of bluegrass by [[Acoustic Guitar (magazine)|''Acoustic Guitar'']].<ref name=\"Acoustic Guitar\">{{cite web|url=http://acousticguitar.com/next-gen-pickers-6-rising-stars-are-carrying-on-the-bluegrass-tradition-by-making-it-their-own/|title=Next-Gen Pickers: 6 Rising Stars are Carrying on the Bluegrass Tradition by Making It Their Own|date=February 16, 2017 |publisher=Acoustic Guitar}}</ref> He also collaborated with [[Molly Tuttle]] on the songs \"Sittin' on Top of the World\" and \"Billy in the Lowground\".\n\n[[File:Strings-fls.jpg|Strings at Bristol Rhythm and Roots Festival, 2017|left|thumb]]\nIn March 2018, [[PBS]] announced that Strings was to be the inaugural performer at the new cave for the renowned series ''[[Bluegrass Underground]]'' in the caverns of [[Pelham, Tennessee]].<ref name=\"The Tennessean\">{{cite web|url=https://www.tennessean.com/story/entertainment/music/2018/03/23/bluegrass-undergrounds-new-home-caverns/432558002/|title=First Look: Bluegrass Underground's New Home The Caverns|publisher=The Tennessean}}</ref><ref name=\"The Grundy County Herald\">{{cite web|url=http://www.grundycountyherald.com/music-and-magic-in-the-pelham-valley/|title=Music and Magic in the Pelham Valley|date=October 6, 2023 |publisher=The Grundy County Herald}}</ref>\n\n=== ''Home'' and ''Renewal'' (2019-2021) ===\nIn January 2019, ''[[Rolling Stone]]'' wrote an article titled \"Why Guitarist Billy Strings Is the Bluegrass Star You Don't Want to Miss.\"<ref name=\"Rolling Stone 3\">{{cite web|url=https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-country/billy-strings-bluegrass-must-see-774144/|title=Why Guitarist Billy Strings Is the Bluegrass Star You Don't Want to Miss|date=January 2, 2019 |magazine=[[Rolling Stone (magazine)|Rolling Stone]]}}</ref> He was signed to [[Rounder Records]] in June 2019.<ref>{{cite web |url=https://bluegrasstoday.com/billy-strings-to-rounder-records/ |title=Billy Strings to Rounder Records |date= June 25, 2019 |first= John |last=Lawless |work= Bluegrass Today}}</ref> Strings released his album ''[[Home (Billy Strings album)|Home]]'' (produced and engineered by Glenn Brown) under the label on September 27, 2019.<ref name=woodward /> It became his most successful release yet, reaching No. 1 on the [[Heatseekers Albums]] and [[Bluegrass Albums]] charts, as well as debuting at No. 11 on the [[Emerging Artists]] Chart.<ref>{{cite magazine |url=https://www.billboard.com/articles/business/chart-beat/8532537/billy-strings-no-11-emerging-artists-chart |title=Billy Strings, Dayseeker and Temples Debut On Emerging Artists Chart |date=October 9, 2019 |first= Xander|last= Zellne |magazine=Billboard}}</ref> On September 26, 2019, Billy Strings was voted the International Bluegrass Music Association Guitar Player of the Year in Raleigh, North Carolina.\n\nHis album ''Home'' won the [[Grammy]] for Best Bluegrass Album at the [[63rd Annual Grammy Awards]].\n\nIn 2021, he joined [[Bill Kreutzmann]]'s [[Billy & the Kids]] for a number of shows, along with James Casey (saxophone).\n\nAs a solo artist, he was part of the [[Newport Folk Festival]] in July 2021.<ref>{{Cite web |title=Newport Folk Festival stage schedule 2021 |url=https://newportfolk.org/2021-schedule/ |access-date=September 21, 2021 |publisher=Newport Folk}}</ref> On September 24, 2021, he released his third studio album, ''[[Renewal (Billy Strings album)|Renewal]]''.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://twitter.com/BillyStrings/status/1420957420990435333|title=New album #RENEWAL out September 24. Preorder now}}</ref><ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.npr.org/2021/09/25/1040669089/grammy-winning-bluegrass-artist-billy-strings-has-a-new-album|title=Grammy Winning Bluegrass Artist Billy Strings Has A New Album|website=NPR.org |access-date=September 28, 2021}}</ref>\n\n===''Me/And/Dad'' (2022-present)===\n\nBilly Strings' touring band was expanded from a quartet to a five piece when frequent guest fiddler Alex Hargreaves was asked to become a permanent member during a show in Baltimore on July 3, 2022.<ref>{{cite web|url=https://www.jambase.com/article/billy-strings-alex-hargreaves-band-member|title=Billy Strings Makes Alex Hargreaves Official Band Member|website=Jambase.com |access-date=September 21, 2023}}</ref> The band consists of Billy Failing (banjo), Royal Masat (bass), Jarrod Walker (mandolin) and Alex Hargreaves (fiddle).\n\nAt the 2022 [[International Bluegrass Music Awards]], Strings was nominated for six awards, winning Entertainer of the Year for the second consecutive year, while \"Red Daisy\" won Song of the Year.<ref>{{Cite web|url=https://www.billboard.com/music/awards/2022-ibma-bluegrass-music-awards-winners-list-1235148100/|title=Billy Strings, B\u00e9la Fleck, Dolly Parton Win Big at 2022 IBMA Bluegrass Music Awards: Winners List|last=Grein|first=Paul|date=September 30, 2022|magazine=[[Billboard (magazine)|Billboard]]|accessdate=October 5, 2022}}</ref>\n\nOn October 3, 2022, Strings announced the album ''Me/And/Dad'', a project with his father Terry Barber.<ref name=\"me/and/dad\">{{Cite web|url=https://www.rollingstone.com/music/music-country/billy-strings-me-and-dad-new-album-1234604449/|title=Billy Strings Picks and Sings With His Father on New Album 'Me/And/Dad'|date=October 3, 2022|last=Freeman|first=Jon|magazine=[[Rolling Stone (magazine)|Rolling Stone]]|accessdate=October 5, 2022}}</ref> While Barber is not his biological father, Strings said about Barber, \"Terry raised me and taught me how to wipe my ass, tie my shoes, and play guitar. That's my fucking dad.\"<ref name=\"me/and/dad\" />\n\nThe album, released in November 2022, is a collection of traditional, country and bluegrass music from [[George Jones]], [[Doc Watson]], [[Hank Thompson (musician)|Hank Thompson]], [[A.P. Carter]] and others. The same day the album was announced, \"Long Journey Home\" / \"[[Life to Go]]\" was released as a two-song single. Barber takes the lead vocal on the Jones-written song \"Life to Go.\"<ref name=\"me/and/dad\" />\n\n==Discography==\n{{Infobox artist discography\n| Artist       = Billy Strings\n| Image        = \n| Caption      = \n| Studio       = 3\n| Live         = 1\n| Compilation  = \n| Music videos = 10\n| EP           = 2\n| Singles      = 10\n| Option name  = As featured artist\n| Option       = 7\n| 1Option name = Collaboration albums\n| 1Option      = 3\n| 2Option name = Other appearances\n| 2Option      = 4\n}}\n\n===Albums===\n====Studio albums====\n{| class=\"wikitable plainrowheaders\" style=\"text-align:center;\"\n|-\n! Strings has received two wins from four nominations.<ref>{{cite web | url=https://americanamusic.org/awards | title=Awards }}</ref>\n{{awards table}}\n|-\n|2020\n|Emerging Artist of the Year\n|rowspan=4|Billy Strings\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|[[2021 Americana Music Honors & Awards|2021]]\n|rowspan=3|Artist of the Year\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|[[2022 Americana Music Honors & Awards|2022]]\n|{{Won}}\n|-\n|[[2023 Americana Music Honors & Awards|2023]]\n|{{Won}}\n{{end}}\n\n=== Grammy Awards ===\nThe [[Grammy Awards]] are awarded annually by the [[National Academy of Recording Arts and Sciences]]. Strings has one win from six nominations.<ref>{{cite web|title=Billy Strings|url=https://www.grammy.com/artists/billy-strings/287520|website=Recording Academy Grammy Awards|access-date=5 February 2024}}</ref>\n\n{{awards table}}\n|-\n|[[63rd Grammy Awards|2021]]\n|rowspan=2|[[Grammy Award for Best Bluegrass Album|Best Bluegrass Album]]\n|''[[Home (Billy Strings album)|Home]]''\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|rowspan=2|[[64th Annual Grammy Awards|2022]]\n|''[[Renewal (Billy Strings album)|Renewal]]''\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n| rowspan=\"2\"|[[Grammy Award for Best American Roots Performance|Best American Roots Performance]]\n|\"[[Renewal (Billy Strings album)|Love and Regret]]\"\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|rowspan=3|[[66th Annual Grammy Awards|2024]]\n|\"California Sober\" <br />{{small|(featuring [[Willie Nelson]])}}\n|{{nom}}\n|-\n|[[Grammy Award for Best Bluegrass Album|Best Bluegrass Album]]\n|''Me/And/Dad''\n|{{nom}}\n|-\n|[[Grammy Award for Best Country Duo/Group Performance|Best Country Duo/Group Performance]]\n|\"High Note\" <br />{{small|([[Dierks Bentley]] featuring Billy Strings)}}\n|{{nom}}\n|-\n{{end}}\n\n=== International Bluegrass Music Awards ===\nThe [[International Bluegrass Music Awards]] are awarded annually by the [[International Bluegrass Music Association]] to honor the best in bluegrass. Strings has won seven<ref>{{Cite web |title=Awards |url=https://americanamusic.org/awards |access-date=2023-10-24 |website=AmericanaMusic.org |language=en}}</ref> awards from 16 nominations.<ref>{{cite web | url=https://thebluegrasssituation.com/read/ibma-awards-2019-see-the-winners/ | title=IBMA Awards 2019: See the Winners | date=September 27, 2019 }}</ref><ref>{{cite web | url=https://thebluegrasssituation.com/read/ibma-awards-2021-see-the-full-list-of-winners/ | title=IBMA Awards 2021: See the Full List of Winners | date=October 2021 }}</ref><ref>{{cite web | url=https://bluegrasstoday.com/marty-raybon-back-to-bluegrass-signs-with-billy-blue-records-2/ | title=2022 IBMA Bluegrass Music Awards nominees announced | date=July 26, 2022 }}</ref>\n\n{{awards table}}\n|-\n|rowspan=2|2019\n|New Artist of the Year\n|rowspan=4|Billy Strings\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|rowspan=2|Guitar Player of the Year\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|rowspan=4|2020\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|Entertainer of the Year\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|Album of the Year\n|''[[Home (Billy Strings album)|Home]]''\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|Instrumental Recording of the Year\n|\"[[Home (Billy Strings album)|Guitar Peace]]\"\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|rowspan=3|2021\n|Guitar Player of the Year\n|rowspan=6|Billy Strings\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|Instrumental Group of the Year\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|rowspan=2|Entertainer of the Year\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|rowspan=6|2022\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|Guitar Player of the Year\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|Instrumental Group of the Year\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|Album of the Year\n|''[[Renewal (Billy Strings album)|Renewal]]''\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|Song of the Year\n|\"[[Renewal (Billy Strings album)|Red Daisy]]\"\n|{{won}}\n|-\n|Instrumental Recording of the Year\n|\"[[Renewal (Billy Strings album)|Ice Bridges]]\"\n|{{nominated}}\n|-\n|2023\n|Entertainer of the Year\n|Billy Strings\n|{{won}}\n{{end}}\n\n== References ==\n<!-- Inline citations added to your article will automatically display here.
